# 徐仙真录

**The True Records of the Xu Immortals**

**Scripture Title:** *The True Records of the Xu Immortals*
Compiled by Fang Wenzhao of the Ming dynasty. Five juan in total.
Base text sourced from the *Wanli Continuation of the Daoist Canon*.

**Preface to *The True Records of the Xu Immortals***

When heaven endows a person with extraordinary talent and they apply it in their time, they may serve their lord with loyalty, protect the people, and attain illustrious rank and honor. Even after death, their temple is venerated in their homeland, and their hidden merits and numinous blessings continue to manifest in later generations—this is no mere coincidence. For heaven must have a purpose for this world and its people, guiding them to such an end. Otherwise, how could they be so unlike others? As for the two Xu Immortals, their case is plainly evident.

The Divine Immortals were natives of Xiong Mountain in Haizhou. They descended from the lineage of King Yan. Their father, Xu Wen, was a renowned general of Wu during the Five Dynasties period. Through his military achievements, he rose through the ranks, eventually being appointed as chancellor. Upon his death, he was posthumously enfeoffed as the Prince of Qi and granted the posthumous title *Zhongwu* ("Loyal and Martial"). All five of his sons received princely titles. Among them were Xu Zhizheng and Xu Zhikua—these were the two Immortals.

During the Tianfu era, the Tang sovereign received the abdication of the throne. Zhizheng was enfeoffed as the Prince of Jiang, and Zhikua as the Prince of Rao. Together, they governed Jinling, their virtue and authority jointly flourishing, bringing peace to the people. They led troops into Min, where the barbarian bandits surrendered, and the borders became tranquil.

The people of Min revered and followed them; thus, they established a shrine to the living deities north of Aofeng, installing statues to honor and serve them. Moved by their sincerity, the deities one day addressed the people of Min: "Next year, I shall depart from you all, but I will silently protect this land." True to their word, the people of Min grew even more devout in their reverence. Whenever disasters struck—droughts, floods, locust plagues, or perils like fires, banditry, and theft—whenever illness, marriages, or funerals required prayers, their supplications were answered as swiftly as an echo. Their literary compositions and calligraphy shone like the radiant Milky Way, dazzling the eyes and ears.

Since the Song dynasty, the deities have received numerous honorific titles. By the Yongle era of our present dynasty, in recognition of their merit in safeguarding the sacred person of the Emperor during his indisposition, their temple was newly renovated. An imperial stele was commissioned, and their posthumous honors were further augmented to manifest the deities' benevolence. From this, we may observe that in life, they were loyal ministers, and in death, enlightened deities—such was the magnitude of their virtue and merit, benefiting both the state and the people. Could this not be the intent of Heaven for this world and its people, that they should attain such a state?

The temple official Fang Wenzhao compiled a record encompassing the deities' lineage, deeds, miraculous manifestations, literary works, and the imperial honors and steles bestowed upon them by previous dynasties and our present court, fearing that with the passage of time, these might fade into obscurity. He titled it *The True Record*. Sun Mengcheng, the military commander of Min and a man of loyalty and righteousness, having read it, was deeply moved. He donated his salary to commission artisans to carve and print the work, ensuring its wider dissemination, and tasked me with writing a preface.

I say: the deities were wise, upright, and just. Their wondrous deeds, both past and present, never strayed into obscurity, eccentricity, or deceit—never did they mislead the world or deceive the people. All their actions were those of loyalty to the sovereign, filial piety to parents, benevolence toward the people, and care for all things—precisely what a scholar-official ought to do. Those who have witnessed or heard of these deeds firsthand need not rely on this compilation to know them. Yet for those in distant and remote regions who have not yet learned of them, this work may serve as a fortunate guide, offering aid in the Way of being a loyal minister and filial child, and in the methods of cultivating oneself and conducting affairs with caution.

Thus, this preface.

Written on an auspicious day in the seventh month of autumn, the eighth year of the Zhengtong era (1443), by Wang Yongsheng, Instructor of the Confucian School of Min County, Fujian.

---

**Preface to the Reprinted *True Record of the Xu Immortals***

*The True Record of the Xu Immortals* chronicles the deeds of the Two Perfected Lords of Vast Grace and Numinous Aid. Within its pages are portraits, the origins of their imperial honors from previous dynasties, and the edicts of our Taizong Wen Emperor commanding the construction of their palace-temple, along with his imperial stele inscriptions. It includes the laudatory verses from *The Book of Hidden Virtue for Doing Good*, the successive imperial decrees of enfeoffment, the rituals of sacrificial offerings, the eulogies of praise, the veneration of successive sages, the bestowal of ceremonial robes, and the literary works composed by the Perfected Lords through spirit-writing—ancient rhapsodies, records, prefaces, poems, divination slips, and resplendent calligraphy—all radiant with celestial brilliance, illuminating past and present.

The temple officials, including Fang Wenzhao, compiled and assembled these materials into two volumes, totaling several scrolls, to record the Perfected Lords' divine transformations in governing the cosmos, their merits in enriching the state and benefiting the people, their silent protection of the sacred person, their unseen support of imperial authority, and their numinous responses and wondrous manifestations—all meticulously documented.

This work was previously printed, but the edition has long since faded beyond recognition and lacks the completeness of the present version. It has now been edited, collated, transcribed, and expanded, and will be reprinted to ensure its wider transmission.

Over the years and months, my strength proved insufficient for the task. Then, by fortunate coincidence, General Sun Jingkang of Mengcheng, the Venerable Guardian of the State, arrived in Futang to assume command of the regional garrison. Soon after taking residence, he personally paid homage to the Perfected Lord at the ancestral temple on Aofeng, offering solemn rites with reverent prostration and libations—whereby the divine and human realms were joined in mutual trust.

Upon the completion of the ceremony, the General took up this compilation and read it with joyous admiration. Moved by its contents, he gladly contributed his own resources, determined to ensure its carving into print, that the Perfected Lord’s numinous traces might be illuminated for eternity. As soon as the project was sheltered under his patronage, he commanded me to compose a preface in commemoration.

I purified myself with fasting and ablutions, lit incense, and pored over the text for days on end. Through this, I came to know that the Perfected Lord’s accumulated merits and boundless benevolence, his utmost loyalty and perfect filial piety, his aspiration for the Dao and attainment of immortality, and his beneficence in aiding all beings—all these true traces are fully preserved within these pages, without omission or lack, destined to endure across ten thousand ages without fading.

Yet, as I reflect upon this, I am struck by a thought: before its carving, the eyes of the world had not seen it, nor had the ears of the world heard of it.

Now that it has been carved, it seems that within the Four Seas and across the Eight Directions, those afflicted with deafness find their ears opened to clarity, and the blind find their eyes restored to sight—nothing remains unseen, nothing unheard. In this world and among its people, those who recite this book and ponder its principles will all come to admire the Perfected Lord’s lofty virtue and distant footsteps, his loyalty and filial piety, his cultivation of virtue and practice of goodness, aspiring to ascend the immortal’s steps and rise to true enlightenment in the Dao.

Thus, it becomes evident that the Perfected Lord not only perfected himself in kalpas past but will also deliver beings in the boundless kalpas to come. Moreover, observing Lord Sun’s intent in this endeavor, one sees his sincere love of goodness, his understanding of the rites of revering Heaven and serving the divine, his loyalty to the sovereign and love for his kin, and his profound veneration of the Perfected Lord’s Dao—qualities no other could match.

Alas! Even a single good thought may move the azure heavens, how much more so when such great goodness as this is manifest? Truly, it deserves to be recorded. The *True Record* is detailed and complete, now forming a comprehensive work. Its opening includes prefaces by the former Right Administration Commissioner of Fujian, Lord Ma of Fengyang, and various scholars; I shall not repeat them here but merely recount the sequence of its concealment and revelation, along with the date of its recent reprinting.

Respectfully inscribed on the fifteenth day of the first lunar month in the year *xinyou* of the Zhengtong reign (1441) by Zhu Hui of Xin’an, humble student and Magistrate of Tong’an County, Quanzhou Prefecture, with hands bowed in reverence.

---

**Preface to the *True Record of Master Xu***

Ninety *li* south of the Three Mountains of Min lies Jin’ao Peak, its towering heights piercing the clouds and soaring beyond the mortal realm. At its foot stands the immortal shrine of the Perfected Lord of Numinous Aid. When I assumed office as regional administrator and took up governance, I once led my subordinates to pay homage at the shrine. Its steps and halls were elegant and refined, the divine countenance solemn and majestic—yet I knew nothing of the deity’s origins.

At that time, a village elder named Huang Heng, nearly eighty years of age, stepped forward and said: "The deity’s surname is Xu, a descendant of King Yan. In the second year of the Kaiyun reign of the Jin (945), he led troops into Min, passing through Jin’ao Peak where he stationed his forces to suppress bandits. Upon attaining immortality, his hidden and manifest powers brought life to the people. A local man, Fang Jue, first established a shrine on Zhiyu Island—now called the Ancestral Temple. Jue’s grandsons, Fang Xun, Fang Gan, and Fang Zhong, reported his deeds to the authorities. The matter reached the imperial court, and an edict was issued. Succeeding generations faithfully upheld their ancestors’ wishes, maintaining the sacrifices with reverence. In the seventh year of the Zhenghe reign of the Song (1117), the sacred flame was divided, and a site was chosen for the present Lingji Palace, where the shrine was built. Later, Fang Huan and Fang Wen led the villagers to expand the main hall and erect new gates. The deity’s numinous power grew daily, manifesting in the sacred dynasty. The temple was renovated, and the sacrificial rites were elevated, outshining all others.

Thereupon, they presented the *True Record of Master Xu*, which exhaustively documents the deity’s miraculous traces, along with the *Literary Works of Master Xu*, all written in the deity’s own hand. Within these texts are steles, biographies, postscripts, prefaces, petitions, eulogies, memorials, as well as the *Rhapsody on Jin’ao Peak* and *In Praise of the Mural Image*—each composition unique, their marvels ever unfolding. The elder then addressed me, saying: 'The deity’s divine manifestations are vivid in the people’s sight, and his hidden merits are fully recorded in these books. Though the books exist, they lack a preface. I humbly request a few words from you.' Unable to refuse, I replied: The deity lived in his era as a man of unparalleled brilliance. Devoted to the study of the Mysterious Dao, he and his kin ascended together to the immortal register, leaving their traces on Jin’ao Peak. The mountain’s vital qi converged in him, and his glory, honored with temple offerings, bestowed blessings upon the region. Why, then, was he long concealed? The fortunes of a land wax and wane, and the workings of destiny alternate between concealment and revelation. The deity foresaw the omens, and though his virtue briefly languished, he vented it through writing—his ethereal brush startling the world, its radiance dazzling the multitude. At times, his numinous power manifested, and prayers were answered as swiftly as an echo. As his cultivated deeds were transmitted and compiled, the records spread through the world, nurturing the vital qi of all beings for over four hundred years.

Now, under the sacred dynasty’s unification, virtue overflows between Hua and Yi. Moved by the deity’s spirit, the court bestowed the title of *Perfected Lord of Numinous Aid*, extolling his hidden virtue and greatly amplifying his divine might. He assists the state and shelters the people, repeatedly bestowing auspicious signs, revered throughout the Four Seas. Thus, his virtue is recorded, his honors grandly proclaimed. With spring and autumn sacrifices, his merits are inscribed on steles, enduring for eternity. At such a propitious juncture, the harmony between deity and humanity is no mere coincidence."

Thus, this preface is written.

Respectfully inscribed on the full moon of the mid-winter month in the year *jiachen* of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1424) by Ma Mian, Grand Master for Thorough Service, Left Administration Commissioner of the Fujian Branch of the Bureau of Transmission.

---

**Volume One of the *True Record of Master Xu***
**The Lineage of the Xu Immortals**

Xu Wen, styled Dunmei, was a native of Qushan in Haizhou. He had five sons: Xu Zhixun, Xu Zhijian, Xu Zhixun, Xu Zhizheng, and Xu Zhie. He also had an adopted son, Xu Zhigao. Initially serving under Yang Xingmi, Wen rose to the rank of Right Guard Commander through merit and began participating in military councils. In the sixth year of the Tianyou reign (909), the Lord of Wu appointed him Prefect of Shengzhou, where he commanded the naval forces at Jinling. In the twelfth year (915), he was promoted to Military Commissioner of Runzhou, Defender-in-Chief of the Zhenhai Army, and Grand Councilor. In the twelfth month of winter, he was enfeoffed as Duke of Qi and concurrently appointed Pacification Commissioner of the Two Zhe. Upon assuming his post in Runzhou, he designated Sheng, Run, Xuan, Chang, Huang, and Chi as the domains of the State of Qi, establishing the Grand Military Prefecture in Shengzhou. In the sixteenth year (919), he was appointed Grand Chancellor and Commander-in-Chief of All Military Affairs. In the fourth year of the Tongguang reign (926), he was posthumously honored as Grand Chancellor. Wen’s four generations of ancestors were enshrined in Jinling. He died in the second year of the Tiancheng reign (927), and the Lord of Wu posthumously conferred upon him the title of King of Qi, with the posthumous name *Zhongwu*. He is now revered as the *Perfected Lord of Loyalty and Brilliance, Supporter of the Qi King*.

Xu Zhigao, styled Zhenglun, later restored his surname to Li and changed his name to Li Sheng. A native of Xuzhou, he was orphaned young and drifted to Haozhou, where Yang Xingmi obtained him as a gift. Wen, a man of warmth and cunning, served as Wu’s Naval Commander, stationing troops in Jinling. Later, for his merits, he was appointed Prefect of Shengzhou. In the fifteenth year of the Tianyou reign (918), Wu appointed him Deputy Military Commissioner and Assistant Administrator, ordering him to resume his title as King of Haidong. In the sixteenth year (919), he was made Left Chief Minister, Participant in Government Affairs, and concurrently Commander of All Military Affairs. In the fourth year of the Tiancheng reign (929), the Lord of Wu appointed Zhigao Grand Councilor. In the second year of the Changxing reign (931), the Lord of Wu stationed Zhigao in Jinling, reappointing him as Military Commissioner of Zhenhai and Ningguo, where he governed Jinling and oversaw all court affairs. In the third year (932), he expanded Jinling’s walls. In the fourth year (933), he constructed palaces in Jinling. In the second year of the Qingtai reign (935), the Lord of Wu appointed Zhigao Grand Marshal, enfeoffed him as King of Qi, and accorded him exceptional rites. In the second year of the Tianfu reign (937), Zhigao established the State of Qi in Jinling. In autumn, Wu’s Crown Prince Lian married Zhigao’s daughter as his consort. Thereupon, he established the Imperial Ancestral Temple and the Altar of Soil and Grain, renaming Jinling as Jiangning Prefecture. In the eighth month of autumn, the Lord of Wu, Pu, presented the abdication decree to King of Qi Zhigao, who then restored his surname to Li, changed his name to Sheng, and honored Xu Wen as his adoptive ancestor. All of Xu’s sons were enfeoffed as kings, and his daughters as commandery and county ladies. He is now revered as the *Tang King of this palace*.

In the first year of the Qianzhen reign (927), Xu Zhixun was appointed by the Lord of Wu as Grand General Assisting the State and Prefect of Jinling, later promoted to Right Commander of the Armies and enfeoffed as Prince of Hong. Xu Zhijian was an adopted son of Xu Wen. While Li Zhigao served the state of Wu with utmost reverence, the other sons of the Xu clan could scarcely tolerate him. They often summoned him to drink, concealing swordsmen with intent to kill him. Zhijian revealed their plot to Zhigao, thus averting the crisis. Later, he was enfeoffed as Prince of E. Xu Zhixun, in the second year of the Tianyou reign (905), was sent by Xu Wen to assist the Lord of Wu in Guangling. The state of Wu appointed him Commander of the Capital Armies, and later enfeoffed him as Prince of Guangling. Xu Zhizheng, in the second year of the Tianfu reign (937), was enfeoffed as Prince of Jiang when Zhigao received the abdication of Wu. In the ninth year of the Tianfu reign (944), he accompanied the Prince of Rao in leading troops into Min, achieving merit at Jin’ao. Thereupon, a temple was established in his honor, and he is now revered as the Perfected Lord of Vast Grace of the Nine Heavens’ Golden Palace.

Xu Zhikua, in the nineteenth year of the Tianyou reign (922), was appointed by the Lord of Wu as Prefect of Jinling. Upon Zhigao’s receipt of the abdication, he was enfeoffed as Prince of Rao, and is now revered as the Perfected Lord of Vast Grace of the Nine Heavens’ Jade Palace.

The Five Marshals were generals stationed at the ancestral temple. The temple stele clearly records their enfeoffed titles. Among the marshals under the Perfected Lords—Yue Wenchang, Xu Wenji, Li Gu, Han Xizai, Zu Quan’en, Yan Keqiu, Ruan Qing, and Lu Qi—though their enfeoffed titles are unrecorded on the stele.

Zha Wenhui, in the eighth year of the Tianfu reign (943), was appointed by the Lord of Tang as Director of the Bureau of Military Affairs. In the ninth year (944), when Lian Chongyu assassinated his lord Wang Yanxi in Min, plunging the region into chaos, Tang dispatched Zha Wenhui under the command of the two princes to lead troops in attacking Jianzhou, stationing at Jianyang. Subsequently, the generals of Min all surrendered. In the eighth month of autumn, Wenhui seized the four prefectures of Jian, Ting, Zhang, and Quan by force of arms. He is now revered as Marshal Zha, the Eternal Guardian of Lingji Palace.

Bian Gao, in the eighth year of the Tianfu reign (943), was appointed by the Lord of Tang as Gentleman for Court Audiences and sent to crush the rebel Zhang Yuxian in Qianzhou, whom he captured and brought back. In the ninth year (944), Gao was appointed Prefect of Xinzhou. In autumn, when great turmoil erupted in Chu, Tang dispatched troops to attack, breaking through Tanzhou. Gao was then appointed Military Commissioner of Hunan, and is now revered as Marshal Bian, the Martial Guardian of Lingji Palace.

Wei Cen, in the ninth year of the Tianfu reign (944), was appointed by the Lord of Tang as Vice Director of the Bureau of Military Affairs. In the twelfth year (947), Cen was sent to pacify Zhang and Quan prefectures, later reassigned as Military Supervisor. He was subsequently appointed Gentleman Attendant to the Crown Prince, and is now revered as Marshal Wei, Left Supervisor of the Military Commission of Lingji Palace.

Zhao Zhong, styled Wende, was initially appointed by the Lord of Tang as Prefect of Chizhou, later recalled and entrusted with the governorship of Xuanzhou. He is now revered as Marshal Zhao, Right Supervisor of the Capital Armies of Lingji Palace.

Diao Yanneng, in the seventh year of the Tianfu reign (942), was appointed by the Lord of Tang as Pacification Commissioner, and is now revered as Marshal Diao, Vanguard Commander of the Capital Armies of Lingji Palace.

*Origins of the Temple’s Founding—Divine Decree and Hexagrams*

In the second year of the Qiandao reign of the Song (1166), Fang Xun, Fang Gan, and Fang Zhong reported these matters to the Prefect, who named it "Divine Response Protecting the Realm." In the third year of the Xining reign (1070), Lin Huaixian and others presented the case to Cheng Shimeng, Minister of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, who memorialized the throne. In the eighth year (1075), Liu Yi and Zhou Ruli again submitted a detailed report. In the first year of the Shaoding reign (1228), Pacification Commissioner Xue Bi memorialized the court. In the first year of the Duanping reign (1234), Zhou Shuo of the Left Treasury and Zhou Sui, Prefect of Chaozhou, presented the matter to Executive Minister Li Zongmian, who memorialized the throne.

In the third year of the Duanping reign (1236), Emperor Lizong of the Song bestowed the following titles:
To the Golden Portal: "Perfected One of the Nine Heavens, Illuminating the Dao, Attaining Virtue, Great Immortal, Manifestly Numinous, Universally Beneficent" (Jiang, King of the River).
To the Jade Portal: "Perfected One of the Nine Heavens, Proclaiming Transformation, Upholding the Teachings, Supreme Immortal, Radiantly Numinous, Vastly Beneficent" (Rao, King of Rao).
To their father: "King of Qi, Perfected One of Loyal Might."
To their mother, née Tian: "Immortal Consort of Benevolent Longevity."
To their consort, née Xu: "Immortal Consort of Compliant Assistance and Sagely Grace."
To their consort, née Tao: "Immortal Consort of Kindly Assistance and Compassionate Beauty."

In the first year of the Jiaxi reign (1237), the temple was bestowed the name "Lingji Palace."

In the fourteenth year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1416), Langzhong Wang Yu and Minister Fang Bin memorialized the throne regarding the deities’ merits. In the following year (1417), Emperor Taizong of the Ming bestowed further titles:
To the Golden Portal: "Perfected One of Pure Subtlety, Penetrating the Mysterious, Tranquil and Void, Wondrously Responsive, Compassionate and Benevolent, Vastly Gracious."
To the Jade Portal: "Perfected One of Lofty Clarity, Vast and Serene, Profoundly Still, Wondrously Responsive, Benevolent and Gracious."
To their father: "Perfected One of Assisting Brilliance."
To their mother: "Immortal Consort of Refined Goodness."
To née Xu: "Immortal Consort of Upright Response."
To née Tao: "Immortal Consort of Reverent Stillness."

In the sixteenth year of the Yongle reign (1418), further titles were bestowed:
To the Golden Portal: "Perfected Lord of Vast Grace, Protecting the State and Sheltering the People."
To the Jade Portal: "Perfected Lord of Vast Grace, Assisting the State and Aiding the People."
To their father: "Perfected Lord of Assisting Brilliance."
The temple was renamed "Hong’en Lingji Palace."

In the tenth year of the Xuande reign (1435), upon the emperor’s ascension, further titles were bestowed:
To the Golden Portal: "Perfected Lord of Vast Grace and Exalted Blessings."
To the Jade Portal: "Perfected Lord of Vast Grace and Descending Blessings."

*The Founding of the Ao Peak Shrine*
In the second year of the Kaiyun reign of the Jin (945), the Perfected Lords led their troops into Min, stationing forces to suppress bandits. The villagers established a shrine at Ao Peak. In the eighth year of the Taiping Xingguo reign of the Song (983), an ancestral temple was rebuilt, with the old site still extant—now the location before the Buddhist hall. By the Jiachen year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1424), 479 years had passed.

*The Hong’en Lingji Ancestral Temple*
In the eighth year of the Taiping Xingguo reign of the Song (983), lay devotee Fang Jue first established the ancestral temple, constructing it of stone. All branch temples trace their origins here, with the Fang clan presiding over seasonal sacrifices. In the seventh year of the Zhizheng reign (1347), Fang Zhuangyou, Fang Fengwu, and Fang Sixi rebuilt it and erected an ancestral temple stele. In the third year of the Yongle reign (1405), Fang Wenzhao and others raised funds to rebuild it, altering its orientation to due north-south. By the Jiachen year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1424), 442 years had passed.

*The Yanxi Community Altar*
In the first year of the Xiangfu reign of the Song (1008), a temple was established at Yanxi, receiving incense from the ancestral temple for worship. In the seventh year of the Zhenghe reign (1117), it was relocated to Hong’en Lingji Palace, and the original site was abandoned—now known as the Community Altar.

*The Hong’en Lingji Palace*
In the seventh year of the Zhenghe reign of the Song (1117), due to the abandonment of Yanxi, the palace was relocated to this site, oriented toward the northeast-southwest. Each year, the village elders led the people to pay homage at the ancestral temple, offering sacrifices of animals and wine to demonstrate their remembrance of origins. In the thirteenth year of the Zhizheng reign (1353), Shen Jianweng and others rebuilt it and erected a stele. Later, Fang Huan, Fang Wen, and others expanded the main hall and erected new gate structures.

In the eighth year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1410), Fang Wenzhao and others raised funds to rebuild it. In the fifteenth year (1417), by imperial decree, eunuchs and local officials led military laborers to expand the foundation and reconstruct the palace, with an imperial inscription for the stele. By the Jiachen year of the Yongle reign (1424), 309 years had passed.

*The Qilong Immortal Shrine*
In the seventh year of the Xuanhe reign of the Song (1125), an altar was first established. Later, prayers for rain were answered, and a shrine was built, hence the name "Praying-for-Rain King." In the eleventh year of the Chunxi reign (1184), villager Guo Xisheng rebuilt it. In the first year of the Yuanzhen reign (1295), Zhang Feng and others renovated it.

By the Jiachen year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (1424), 300 years had passed.

**Origins of the Incense Flame**
The incense flame of Lingji spread throughout the region. In those days, with the flourishing of people and places, temples were established everywhere—such as the Jade Water and Clear River Osmanthus Palace, Yue Mountain and Jade Slope, Fu Mountain and Qilong, and the Stone Abode at the end of Xin’an River. To the west of Peng Mountain and Qingbu, from north to south as far as White Deer, encompassing the Cishi (Maitreya) Blessing Treasury of Heshan, the Dharma-Protecting *Kalyāṇa*-Spirits—though their halls and temples differed, their incense flames all traced back to the ancestral palace, for in truth they sprang from the same source: the Vast Grace True Lord.

The generals and marshals of each shrine varied, having been dispatched by the True Lord at the time of each temple’s founding.

**The Legend of Immortal Xu**
In the ancestral shrine of Lingji Palace, though the eastern and western corridors had fallen into ruin, the murals stood firm. In my youth, I often heard scholars say: these depict the deeds of the two Xu Immortals, whose temple offerings were established. The events are recorded in sequence, their truth preserved, and poems appended to praise their virtue.

From their early devotion to goodness and scripture-holding to their hidden merits in aiding others, from the founding of the Zhiyu Temple to the spreading of incense flames, each step followed in order. Entering this hall, one gazes upon centuries of bygone affairs as if they were present. Today, all who come cannot but revere and stand in awe.

As the years passed, the murals faded and dimmed, ever feared to be lost to oblivion. Thus, I have recorded them in full within this book, to be passed down for endless generations.

Moreover, in the reign of the Great Ming’s Yongle Emperor, their numinous efficacy and manifest responses shone forth—revering Heaven and diligently serving the people—to carry on their legacy. This, too, is the Immortals’ divine power, revived in the Yongle era.

Respectfully inscribed by Huang Heng, the Hermit of Linqiu, with bowed head.

**Devotion to Goodness and Cultivation of Conduct**
The Two Immortals were descendants of King Yan of Xu, sons of Xu Wen of the Southern Tang, and natives of Qushan in Haizhou. From youth, they cherished goodness and steadfastly pursued virtuous conduct. Whenever they encountered the poor and distressed, they gladly gave alms. In daily life, they disdained extravagance, valuing neither rare treasures nor frivolous amusements. Content with simplicity, they often ate plain meals at dawn. Thus, their merit and practice grew daily, leading them to ascend the Dao’s shore.

*Poem:*
Good causes and fruits are rare in the world,
Who knows the joy of cultivating the heart for the Dao?
The cinnabar fire burns the sky in a lovable place,
Their manifest blessings endure from ancient times to now.

**Holding Scriptures and Worshipping the Dipper**
At dawn, they ate plain meals and recited the *Jade Emperor Sutra*, sacred invocations, and the *Northern Dipper Longevity Sutra*. Esoteric rituals and secret texts were all thoroughly practiced. Each night, they fasted, bathed, and set up an incense altar. Only with their younger brother did they pray to Heaven and worship the Dipper, vowing to end warfare and pacify the people. Though state affairs were pressing, they never wavered in their resolve, ever diligently turning toward the Dao, their devotion deepening over time.

*Poem:*
Who knows their daily diligence in seeking the Dao?
Holding scriptures and worshipping the Dipper, they amassed merit.
When cultivation is complete and conduct perfected, they transcend the Three Realms,
Their names inscribed in the Immortals’ register for ten thousand ages.

**Enfeoffed as Kings to Pacify the Realm**
In the second year of Tianfu during the Later Jin’s abdication to the Tang (Dingyou, 757), the younger brother, Zhi Zheng, was enfeoffed as King of Jiang, and Zhi E as King of Rao. They were stationed in Jinling to soothe the displaced and defend over twenty prefectures. Taxes were lightened for the people, and provisions were ample for the troops. The barbarians of the forests and caves were subdued, holding fast to the essentials of governance and defense.

Young and old alike frolicked and played, hand in hand singing songs of joy—thus the people of Jiangnan all found peace and stability.

*Poem:*
Fragrant blossoms stand united in meritorious deeds,
Enfeoffed with lands and titles as kings they received.
The land at peace, the people prosperous, songs resound,
Guarding Jinling with loyalty, their devotion profound.

**Elders Welcoming the Master**
In the second year of Kaiyun during the Later Jin (Yisi, 945), internal strife erupted within the Min kingdom under King Wang. Warfare raged unchecked. Yu led Generals Zha, Bian, and ten thousand cavalry into Min, stationing troops south of the Xiajiang. As they passed through Qingbu, the village elders offered baskets of food and jars of drink, lining the road to welcome them. The people of both Xiang and Min found refuge, living undisturbed in their homes. Not a hair was harmed, and all were able to live in peace.

*Poem:*
Yu stationed his troops south of Xiajiang’s gorge,
Elders welcomed him with reverence, their safety restored.
No fowl nor hound was startled, all remained secure,
Without harm, officials and people rejoiced as one.

**Stationing Troops to Eliminate Bandits**
The elders, having welcomed the master, voiced their grievances to the king: "Recently, several hundred elite soldiers have concealed themselves atop Aofeng Peak, acting with arrogance and violence, on the verge of causing chaos. Now that Your Majesty’s forces have arrived among the villages, neighboring communities suffer deeply from their disturbances and cannot live in peace." Moved by their plight, the king led his troops southward, subdued the peak, and annihilated the ringleaders without battle. The people all returned to their homes in peace.

*Poem:*
Bandits atop Aofeng Peak struck fear profound,
Neighboring lands suffered, no peace could be found.
With a single deployment, the elite forces swept clean,
And ten thousand people resumed their livelihoods serene.

**Erecting a Shrine to Offer Longevity Prayers**
Once the bandits were pacified, the master returned. The elders, filled with admiration and reluctance to part, could not bear to let him go. They cut thatch to build a hut north of Aofeng’s Dragon Pool, where they enshrined his image, offering morning incense and evening lamps to pray for the king’s longevity. The king, his heart touched, swore to the people: "Next year, I shall depart this world and reside here." The site of that old shrine is now before the Buddhist abode.

*Poem:*
Grateful for boundless grace, their devotion ran deep,
North of Aofeng’s pool, a shrine they did keep.
There, they pledged to the villagers with solemn vow,
That one day, his spirit would dwell among them now.

**Appointed as Directors of the Celestial Waters**
The two kings lived in the world with unwavering devotion to cultivation. Their merits complete and conduct perfected, their Dao profound and virtue mysterious, their names ascended to the celestial courts and resounded in the Dipper’s palace. That year, they transcended in the constellation of Gouwu. Due to their lifelong worship of the Dipper, the brothers’ names were jointly inscribed as the two Directors of the Dipper’s Waters. They entered and exited the heavenly palaces, holding a green tally in the left hand and wielding a yellow axe in the right, standing in attendance before the Emperor to silently protect the people below.

*Poem:*
The Dipper’s palace guards its secrets well,
Yet immortals, through the Dao’s mysteries, their truths tell.
First as Directors of the Celestial Waters they rose,
Entering jade palaces, serving the Emperor’s repose.

**The Numinous Treasure Expounds the Scriptures**
Having attained the Dao, Youzhen journeyed to the Five Luminaries Palace to pay homage to the Numinous Treasure Heavenly Worthy. Kneeling, he addressed the deity: "By fortune from past lives, I have been blessed to be counted among the world’s beings. Gazing below at the Desire Realm, I see many commit evil deeds without shame or remorse, unaware of awakening. By what karmic conditions may they be saved? I beseech your compassion." The Heavenly Worthy then expounded the scriptural verses: "Those who cultivate themselves shall ever dwell in auspicious blessings."

*Poem:*
The Mystic Gate’s teachings from ancient times unfold,
Its sacred texts imparted, their truths untold.
Only these two immortals grasped the Dao’s profound way,
In the Five Luminaries Palace, scriptures they did sway.

**The Yellow Court’s Secret Teachings**
Though the kings were born into the mortal realm and enfeoffed with royal titles, they did not cling to the honor and glory of their rank. Instead, they delighted in the Dao, exhaustively studying the classics, dwelling in purity and embracing simplicity. Even after their merits were complete and their names inscribed in the immortals’ register, the Master of the Yellow Court, having long admired them, sought their secret teachings. During the Kaiyun era of the Later Jin, he visited the Yellow Court Daoist and received the *Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue* on the secrets of longevity.

*Poem:*
What was the Yellow Court Daoist’s intent?
The secret of longevity the world could not prevent.
Having reached the abode where the perfected dwell,
Many scriptures and mysteries to them did swell.

**Paying Homage to the Buddha of Steadfast Light**
After the two kings returned to the Dao, they greatly propagated the secrets of emptiness and mystery, fully realizing their natural wonders. Above, they aided the Dipper’s immortals; below, they benefited all beings. Later, they paid homage to the Buddha of Steadfast Light, the Bodhisattva of Universal Compassion and Salvation, directly entering the realm of Suchness. Their minds aligned with the divine mechanism, their inner wisdom’s power flourished, further illuminating the principle of true emptiness and wondrous being—all was naturally attained.

*Poem:*
Before the ancient Buddha’s altar, in meditation deep,
True immortals bowed in reverence, the Chan gate to seek.
Mind realized, nature seen—emptiness within being,
Daoist and Buddhist truths, in essence, are one and the same.

**Talismans and Medicines to Aid the People**
In the second year of Xiande during the Later Zhou (Yimao, 955), the king began to issue talismanic judgments and prescribe medicines to aid the people. His fame spread in all directions, and his hidden merits lingered in the ears and eyes of the people. They revered and loved him as if he were their parents, relying on his protection. From then on, those who sought divine assistance or inquired of affairs found their prayers answered without fail. The sick were healed, and chronic illnesses vanished. His divine virtue grew ever more manifest, and the people’s hearts turned toward him in devotion.

*Poem:*
In Xiande’s reign, talismans spread far and wide,
With wondrous remedies, countless lives they did guide.
No plea went unanswered, his power truly divine,
The aged walked in health, the ill in peace did shine.

**Reversing the Wind to Extinguish Fire**
During the Xiande era, the villagers’ carelessness led to the disaster of Zaihuo. A single spark ignited, fanned by the wind into a raging blaze. Fierce and scorching, it consumed homes and dwellings. Towering beams and grand halls vanished in an instant, yet the flames continued to spread unchecked. The people, pale with fear, were at a loss, crying out to the two kings and begging for salvation. The kings then summoned a reverse wind with a single gust, extinguishing the flames entirely. The people all felt the depth of their virtue.

*Poem:*
A raging fire consumed the homes with might,
Grand halls and lofty beams vanished in its light.
With a divine gust, the wind reversed its course,
The people lived in peace, their livelihoods secure.

**Rescuing Boats at Cang Gorge**
The rivers and streams allowed for boat travel, with Min’s waterways being the most treacherous. The waters of Cang Gorge were especially perilous. Jagged rocks and towering cliffs lined the winding currents, causing many boats to be wrecked and stranded.

In the first year of the *Kaibao* reign of the Song, *wuchen* (8th year of the sexagenary cycle), a merchant vessel laden with goods nearly capsized. The boatmen cried out to the two kings, praying for protection. Suddenly, a faint yellow banner appeared amidst the clouds, and the boat sailed smoothly without peril.

*Poem:*
The Min’s waters, treacherous, bar the way,
Cang Gorge’s trials bring no small dismay.
Yet at their cries, a banner dimly shown,
The waves grew still, the currents gently flown.

**Dispelling Misfortune**
In the eighth year of the *Kaibao* reign, *dinghai* (24th year of the sexagenary cycle), during mid-spring, the seasonal *qi* was unbalanced. Villagers fell ill with pestilential sickness, entire households stricken in succession. Physicians’ treatments proved ineffective, and the affliction spread, some calling it the "toad miasma." The neighboring hamlets grew uneasy. They prayed at the shrine, where divine talismans and the wondrous remedies of the Celestial Physician were bestowed, bringing swift and miraculous cures. Thus were composed the *"Rhapsody on Dispelling Pestilence"* and the *"Incantation for Driving Away Toad Miasma."*

*Poem:*
The noxious vapors spread, unbearable their might,
Neighbors and lanes alike were filled with dread and fright.
By numinous power, the gales swept clean the air,
And peace was restored, the people’s health laid bare.

**Erecting a Shrine on Mushroom Isle**

In the eighth year of Xingguo of the Song (983), spring brought no rain. Prayers to the king were swiftly answered, and the villagers approached the shrine to seek divine response. Suddenly, a wooden incense burner emerged from the water’s heart, borne aloft by twin dragons. It flowed westward to Mushroom Isle, where it halted, spanning the stream without advancing. Paper money marked the boundary, and an extraordinary fragrance filled the air. That night, celestial lanterns appeared as an auspicious sign. The villager Fang Jue thus established the shrine there. Later, when sacred lingzhi mushrooms grew upon the isle, it was named *Zhiyu* (Mushroom Isle).

*Poem:*
A divine censer rose, encircled by twin dragons,
Borne westward to rest upon the isle.
Celestial lanterns shone through the night, all omens fulfilled—
Thus the villagers built the shrine’s hall.

**Hall of Harmonious Kinship**
The divine father’s name was Wen, who served as Prime Minister under the Wu. He passed in the *dinghai* year of Tiancheng (927) and was posthumously enfeoffed as Prince Qi, with the posthumous title *Zhongwu* (Loyal and Martial). His mother, Lady Tian, named Shu, was reverently honored as the Immortal Consort *Renshou Xianfei* (Benevolent and Longevous). When the two princes manifested their divine power and the shrine was established, the Hall of Harmonious Kinship was first erected to honor them. Arrayed within were King Tang, King Hong, King E, and King Guangling—all elder brothers of the deities. Dukes, marquises, grandsons, and sons—all were the immortal kin of the kings.

*Poem:*
The twin kings’ shrine stands, guarding southern Min,
A separate hall built to honor their parents.
In harmonious robes, as if still living,
Processions and dances in vibrant new garments.

**Establishing the Hall of Life-Registration**
The lady’s nature was gentle and yielding, her virtue akin to Earth. She diligently upheld the duties of a wife, her inner power aiding in hidden merits. Consort Xu of Prince Jiang, named Zhen, was enfeoffed as Lady of Jing State and honored as the Immortal Consort *Shunzhu Renhui Xianfei* (Compliant and Benevolent). Consort Tao of Prince Rao, named Huanyun, was enfeoffed as Lady of Wei State and honored as the Immortal Consort *Shanzhu Ciyi Xianfei* (Kindly and Auspicious). Together, they received the Daoist precepts of the Eastern Flowery Law Lord and were jointly entrusted with the affairs of the Bureau of Life-Prolongation and the Bureau of Life-Registration.

*Poem:*
Her inner power in governance shone most clearly,
In one transformation, her merits were fulfilled.

**Establishment of the Hall of Life-Registration**
The art of Life-Registration was once transmitted by the Eastern Flowery Lord, hence the hall was built and so named.

**Celestial Edicts of the Heavenly Emperor**
In the first year of *Zhidao* of the Song (995 CE), the villagers established the Yellow Register Assembly. Suddenly, celestial music resounded through the heavens, and auspicious clouds swirled about. The Heavenly Emperor dispatched divine messengers to bestow edicts, enfeoffing the Prince of Jiang as *True Man of the Golden Portal* and the Prince of Rao as *True Man of the Jade Portal*, each with a sixteen-character title. The consorts Tao and Xu were honored as *Immortal Consorts*, and their divine parents were likewise granted celestial enfeoffment. Thus, the hagiographies of the Two Perfected Ones were completed.

*Poem:*
The Yellow Register banquet opened as music filled the skies,
Divine messengers bestowed edicts, their grace profound.
The twin portals’ fame endures through endless ages,
A household of pearls and jade receives Heaven’s seal.

**Divine Soldiers Defend the Realm**
When the Min native Liu Yi was stationed in Guizhou, bandits besieged the border. Yi silently prayed to the Twin Portals, and the bandits indeed retreated. Later, when Han Shizhong fought at Dayi Garrison and Wu Zhen at Heshang Plain, divine soldiers were dispatched to shield the nation. They manifested the numinous power of Mount Gong’s grasses and trees, making the enemy see a million iron-clad cavalry in strict formation—so daunting that the foe fled without battle.

*Poem:*
His might displayed in Guizhou, repelling the southern tribes,
The gods’ efficacy shown in the mountain’s grasses and trees.
With iron cavalry arrayed in the valleys,
The bandits were swiftly pacified.

**Founding of Longshou Palace**
In the seventh year of *Zhenghe* of the Song (1117 CE), a site was divined at Dragon’s Head, and a temple was erected. In the *guisi* year of the Yuan (1293 CE), Shen Jianweng and others restored it, while Fang Huan and Fang Wen further erected gate tablets. In the *gengyin* year (1350 CE), funds were raised for reconstruction, though it remained unfinished. In the *dingyou* year of *Yongle* of the Ming (1417 CE), an imperial edict expanded the grounds, and the temple was rebuilt anew. The court appointed Fang Wenzhao as the sacrificial officer to oversee its worship.

*Poem:*
Fujian’s southern land, mighty beneath Ao Peak,
Here the Dragon’s Head Palace was chosen and built.
For the nation’s great merit, imperial favor runs deep,
Honored with a temple, its blessings endless.

**Auspicious Response to Prayers for Rain**
In the seventh year of *Xuanhe* of the Song (1125 CE), autumn brought no rain. The fields withered, and the harvest was imperiled. Farmers and villagers joined hands, praying at the temple. Suddenly, clouds and mist filled the sky, and a torrential downpour followed. From afar, on the southern mountain, a divine dragon flickered into view, bearing a yellow banner emblazoned with golden characters. The year yielded a bountiful harvest, and the people rejoiced in peace.

*Poem:*
Drought parched the land, the fields lay barren,
Crops withered, no water to be found.
At the temple’s prayer, dragon-mist arose,
The yellow banner gleamed, comforting the farmers.

**Eradicating Locusts**
In the second year of *Jiading* of the Song (1209 CE), during the fifth lunar month, the fields’ grain had tasseled but not yet ripened when locusts swarmed the land, wreaking havoc. The people were helpless. The village elders gathered at the temple, burning incense and offering solemn prayers with blood-stained sincerity. The Two Immortals summoned swift thunder, fierce rain, and violent winds, blowing the pests to the ground, where they perished.

*Poem:*
The western fields’ millet stood tall,
When locusts arose in grim despair.
Great winds and thunder brought torrential rain,
The pests fell dead, filling the furrows.

**Divine Soldiers Tame the Flood**
The villagers had long ago built a temple by Yan Brook, but it fell into ruin. In the fifth month of the *wuzǐ* year of *Shaoding* of the Song (1228 CE), heavy rains caused the brook to swell violently, drowning the crops. The people were terrified, but suddenly, a thousand laborers appeared, wielding baskets and spades. They dredged the source and diverted the flow, and the waters ceased to threaten the people. Soon after, the laborers vanished—revealed to be divine soldiers.

*Poem:*
Yan Brook’s floods surged, drowning the fields,
The people stood helpless, filled with dread.
Suddenly, a thousand men bore their tools,
Only then did they know the gods had come.

**Imperial Edicts and Honors**
The Two Immortals had earlier received the Heavenly Emperor’s favor and edicts of honor, their deeds renowned in their time, vivid in the people’s memory. Yet as temple steles crumbled, their story faded. In the third year of *Duanping* of the Song (1236 CE), the villagers petitioned the court, and an imperial edict was issued, bestowing the sixteen-character titles. The entire temple received imperial grace. In the first year of *Jiaxi* (1237 CE), the temple was honored with the golden-script edict plaque *Lingji Palace*.

*Poem:*
The Heavenly edicts came long ago,
Their divine traces later memorialized to the throne.
The golden-script plaque *Lingji* bestowed,
Their fame eternal, never to fade.

**Imperial Command to Welcome the Deities**

In the *Bingshen* year of *Yongle* of the Great Ming (1416 CE), Minister of War Fang Bin and Commander Lin Guan reported the deities’ merits to the court. A special command was issued, and Secretary Wang Yu was dispatched bearing the imperial decree to the temple, where he addressed the gods: “Born as nobles and enfeoffed as kings, you shed your mortal forms to ascend as immortals, receiving offerings in this land. Now we command the welcoming of your spirits—may you manifest your divine might.” The gods responded with resonant assent and graciously consented. Thereupon, they accompanied Zeng, Chen, and Sun to the capital.

*Poem:*
Throughout court and countryside, their numinous power was long renowned;
Noble ministers bore the decree to greet them with reverence.
May the gods’ brush grant their blessing in answer,
As crane-borne chariots soar to the imperial capital.

**The Two Immortals’ Audience at Court**
Once the imperial edict was issued, the gods’ efficacy had already been displayed at court. On the eleventh day of the third month of *Dingyou* in *Yongle* (1407 CE), the Two Immortals, leading the Five Marshals, appeared in the vermilion steps amid auspicious mists, manifesting their true forms. Their divine validation thus confirmed, reverence deepened, and from that time onward, incense and offerings flourished daily. The elder and younger brothers, their glory conjoined, and the entire temple’s retinue received grand honors, and so the rites of worship were renewed.

*Poem:*
Auspicious mists swirled as they revealed their true forms,
In this enlightened age, they came to court to meet the sovereign.
Long-ordained karmic convergence was no mere chance—
Their loyal service in aiding the state lay in revering the gods.

**Presenting the Divine Elixir**
That year, the emperor’s health faltered, and the gods, ever vigilant, never left his side. Silently, they activated their transformative power, greatly manifesting their divine favor: they bestowed sacred talismans and healed him with the wondrous medicines of Heaven’s physicians. With every word, efficacy followed; their response was as swift as an echo. His body was soon restored, and his chronic ailments, cast off, returned him to health. Moved by the gods’ turning of fate and the gift of rebirth,

*Poem:*
Sacred talismans and wondrous medicines from Heaven’s physicians,
Ever guarding the imperial person, never for a moment apart.
The gods’ power in curing illness is beyond repayment—
The grace of rebirth is known in this heart alone.

**Establishing a Temple in the Capital**
For their divine machinations in aiding the state, a traveling temple was built in Beijing as a dwelling for the gods. Eminent Daoists were assembled, and officials such as Zeng, Chen, and Sun were specially appointed to oversee the rites. To the west of the temple, the *Zhusheng Hall* was constructed to honor the *Xianfei* of Jing and Wei. At times, five-colored auspicious clouds and propitious mists appeared, and white cranes descended in spirals, their divine responses manifesting in omens.

*Poem:*
Grateful for the gods’ transformative power,
A traveling temple was built as their divine abode.
Auspicious signs and responses revealed their numinous favor—
Their fame, eternal as sun and moon.

**Imperial Decree of Ennoblement**
For their resplendent divine merit, the gods were ennobled:
The *Golden Portal of the Pure and Subtle, Cavernous and Mysterious, Lofty and Void, Wondrously Responsive, Compassionate and Benevolent, Protector of the State and Shelter of the People, Vast Grace True Lord*;
The *Jade Portal of the Lofty and Bright, Vast and Tranquil, Profound and Serene, Wondrously Responsive, Benevolent and Kind, Aider of the State and Guardian of the People, Vast Grace True Lord*.
The gods’ father was ennobled as the *Yuliang True Lord*;
Their mother as the *Shushan Immortal Consort*;
The *Jingguo Zhenying Immortal Consort*;
The *Weiguo Gongjing Immortal Consort*.

*Poem:*
Long honored by jade seals, now glorified by imperial decree,
Again we witness the Ming court’s new bestowals.
The blessings of the ennobling edict endure forever—
Thirty-two characters, renowned through ages past and present.

**Filial Piety Moves the Gods**
Zhu Guanyinbao, a pearl-driller from Jiangning County in Yingtian Prefecture, had traveled to Beijing for work, longing for his parents. One day, he heard his mother had developed a boil behind her ear. Pierced with sorrow, he hastened to *Lingji Palace* and performed three thousand prostrations. The True Lord graciously manifested, descending talismans and medicines to heal her instantly. The court, praising his filial devotion, bestowed honors upon his household and recorded his story.

*Poem:*
Longing for his parents filled his dreams,
Hearing of her illness, he prayed to the True Lord.
Filial piety moved the gods to respond—
His household was honored, his tale inscribed.

**Establishing the Altar and Auspicious Responses**
On the fifteenth day of the second month of *Jihai* in the Great Ming (1419 CE), a fast was held. The court dispatched eunuch Zhang Da, Master of Rites, bearing the seal, and Daoist Master Zhang Yuqing to gather over seven thousand Daoists from Zhejiang, Huguang, Jiangxi, and Fujian. Within seven days, immortals manifested, vanishing without a trace. White cranes descended upon the altar, and multicolored clouds gathered in auspicious assembly.

*Poem:*
Upon the sacred peak’s hall, the altar was raised;
White cranes and auspicious clouds heralded the immortals’ descent.
The temple’s retinue received imperial favor, their devotion laid before the throne—
The Supreme Mystery deigned to listen, bestowing boundless grace.

**Imperial Composition: *The Biographies of the Two Perfected as Immortals***

Xu Zhizheng, descended from the lineage of King Yan, was a native of Southern Tang during the Five Dynasties period. Alongside his younger brother Zhie, both were peerlessly heroic and outstanding, embodying magnanimity and benevolence toward all things, loyalty to their lord, and filial piety to their parents. They revered the Three Treasures and shared a common devotion to goodness. Diligent in the pursuit of the supreme Dao, they performed fasts and offered jiao rituals, daily reciting the *True Scripture of the Jade Emperor* and the sacred invocations.

Both brothers served the state of Wu, with Zhizheng later enfeoffed as Prince of Jiang and Zhie as Prince of Rao, governing Jinling. Their military preparedness was thorough, and they comforted and resettled refugees, securing over ten prefectures. Taxes were lightened, and military provisions were abundant. The tribes of the forests and caves adhered to their governance, holding fast to the covenants. The young and old frolicked together, joining hands in song. No grievances arose from above, nor enmities from below. The people of Jiangnan all relied upon them for peace.

Later, when ordered to lead troops to attack Min, fearing the suffering of the innocent, they made a solemn vow: with each watch of the night, they faced north to pay homage to the Dipper, and their morning meals were always vegetarian, praying for the cessation of war. Wherever they went, they labored to comfort the people, and the prefectures surrendered at the mere rumor of their approach. Elders offered baskets of food and pitchers of drink, crowding the roads to welcome them.

At Xiajiang Gorge, several hundred deserters took refuge in the mountains and stirred rebellion. They promptly ordered the extermination of the ringleaders, and the remaining soldiers cast aside their armor, shouting and dancing in surrender. The people all dwelt in peace, and though the troops passed through fields and gardens, not a single vegetable or grain of rice was violated. The villagers then conferred among themselves, saying: "These are the parents who have given us new life." They erected a shrine to them on Aofeng Peak to offer sacrifices.

Before long, the brothers ascended as immortals in succession, becoming the Water Commissioners of the Dipper’s Central Bureau, coming and going from the Celestial Palace. Through accumulated deeds and merits, they silently protected the common folk, enabling all to fulfill loyalty and filial piety and to revere the Three Treasures. In times of drought, the people prayed at the shrine. Before the shrine lay a deep pool, its depths unfathomable. Suddenly, a divine serpent emerged, coiling upon the altar table, its color like cinnabar. Clouds rose from the pool’s surface, lingering all day without dispersing. The next morning, rain indeed fell, and the year saw a great harvest.

One day, torrential rains caused the Yanxi River to swell violently, submerging fields and crops. The people, panicked and at a loss, suddenly saw a thousand laborers competing with baskets and spades, dredging the source and diverting the flow, so that the waters no longer harmed the people. Soon after, they vanished without a trace—it was known then that these were divine soldiers.

The rocks of Cangxia Rapids were steep and treacherous; boats ascending or descending often lost control, and the boatmen cried out in prayer. Then, banners were faintly seen in the clouds, and the waters immediately calmed without incident. Whenever a household was careless with fire, or locusts damaged the crops, the people would appeal to them, and the wind would reverse to extinguish the flames, or the locusts would fly away of their own accord. They also crafted talismanic medicines to distribute among the people; all who suffered from illness and took them recovered immediately. Those who prayed for children took the medicine and soon bore descendants in abundance and prosperity. Thus, the people lived in peace, the land flourished, and the villages remained tranquil.

The people of Min, Liu Yi, governed Guizhou and Jiaozhi when barbarian rebels invaded the frontier, capturing Qin and Lian prefectures. Overwhelmed with dread, Liu Yi knew not what to do; he fasted, purified himself, and prayed, and the rebels indeed scattered and fled. Later, when Han Shizhong fought at Dayi Garrison and Wu Jie at Heshang Plain, both dispatched generals and soldiers to defend the realm, invoking the numinous power of the grasses and trees of Mount Bagong, so that the enemy troops saw from afar banners and standards fluttering, valleys filled with a million iron-clad cavalry, ranks arrayed in solemn order, the thunder of war drums shaking the plains. Before battle was joined, the foe had already fled in defeat. Their divine merits and wondrous manifestations are too numerous to recount in full.

One day, during the Yellow Register Fast, celestial music suddenly resounded in the sky, auspicious clouds swirled, and immortal officials led their retinue to the capital. Thereupon, moved by their devotion, the Heavenly Emperor dispatched divine messengers to confer investiture: Jiang Wang was enfeoffed as the Nine Heavens Golden Palace Illuminating Dao and Manifesting Virtue Great Immortal, Manifest Numinous and Universally Beneficent Perfected, charged with overseeing the Upper Clarity Celestial Astronomy Bureau, empowered to act at his discretion, and to correspond with the Wenchang Controller of Records and the Heavenly Mechanism Secretariat. His wife, Lady Xu of Jing State, was enfeoffed as the Compassionate and Benevolent Immortal Consort Assisting in Virtue, concurrently overseeing the Right Perfected Bureau of Great Unity’s Longevity.

Rao Wang was enfeoffed as the Nine Heavens Jade Palace Propagating Transformation and Upholding the Dao Supreme Immortal, Manifest Numinous and Vastly Beneficent Perfected, charged with overseeing all bureaus of the Lower Earthly Prison, empowered to act at his discretion, and concurrently serving as the Prime Minister of the Southern Culmen’s Life-Registering Bureau and the Secretary of the Thunder and Mystery Secretariat. His wife, Lady Tao of Wei State, was enfeoffed as the Kindly and Gracious Immortal Consort Assisting in Virtue, concurrently overseeing the Northern Yin Life-Registering Bureau of the Primordial Sovereign.

Their father was enfeoffed as the Illustrious and Bright Perfected, and their mother as the Virtuous and Kind Immortal Consort. Former titles were retained as before.

New temples were established in Min, and an auxiliary shrine was built in the capital to house the deities, with special edicts commanding officials to reverently maintain the sacrifices. As ministers whose merit extended to the altars of state and whose beneficence spread over the people, they were born as heroes and died as luminous spirits. Even a hundred generations hence, they continue to bless the living and secretly assist the imperial transformation.

Consider Zhi Zheng and Zhi E: scions of noble houses, they served their lord in a declining age, yet were able to pacify a troubled region and preserve its people. When they marched into Min, they entered with banners still, eradicated the rebels, delivered the people from the horrors of war, and restored them to the peace of their homes. Thus, the people’s hearts were moved with gratitude, and though they could not fully repay such kindness, they erected living shrines to honor them. After their ascension as immortals, they averted fires, warded off great calamities, spared the people from famine and pestilence, and granted them the joy of rest and renewal. Their merits complete and conduct perfected, they moved the heart of Heaven, which bestowed upon them the title of Perfected and ranked them among the immortals. Recently, through their subtle operation of transformative mechanisms, they greatly manifested numinous favor, curing Our illness. Therefore, We add honorific titles, renovate their temples, and reverently offer sacrifices in gratitude for their divine blessings.

All this arises from the accumulation of hidden merits, and thus their luminous glory endures, as eternal as Heaven and Earth—how could this be mere coincidence?

The poem says:
Serving parents with filial piety, serving the lord with loyalty,
Their fragrant blossoms intertwined—who could compare?
Further diligent in cultivating the true Dao,
They shared the fief’s soil and received the king’s enfeoffment.

Another:
In those years, they stationed troops toward Jinling,
The lands south of the river knew not war.
The army entered the seven Min regions, the people at peace,
How much blessing and grace bestowed upon the living!

Another:
In drought, the numinous pool stirred the coiled dragon,
Great rains poured forth, bringing a bountiful year.
The Huang Creek’s waters receded, the people free from harm,
The Cangxia Rapids’ boats passed through peril unscathed.

Another:
Locusts harmed not the crops, fires wrought no disaster,
Fields and wilds alike received abundant grace.
They bestowed divine talismans to multiply descendants,
And cured grave illnesses, restoring the stricken.

Another:
In Gui Prefecture, they manifested power to repel the barbarian foe,
Displaying wonders like those of Mount Bagong.
A million divine soldiers filled the valleys,
Dissolving the siege in an instant.

Another:
Divine talismans and wondrous medicines from celestial physicians,
Guarding Our humble self without moment’s departure.
The healing power of their divine merit is beyond repayment,
This heart knows the gratitude of rebirth.

Another:
Responding to prayers without delay,
Their transformative power partakes of Creation’s mechanism.
Riding qi and wind, they roam the eight directions,
Descending from celestial palaces to manifest numinous might.

Another:
Born a renowned general, they built great merit,
Their conduct perfected, they ascended as immortals—fortune has its cause.
In the capital’s Ao Peak, their temple receives eternal offerings,
Their cinnabar script titles attest to their celestial authenticity.

Another:
Their majestic numinous power shines ever brighter,
Protecting the state and pacifying the people, their merits grow ever more manifest.
Not only does their fame illuminate sun and moon,
But forever they endure with Earth and Heaven.

Imperially Composed Stele for the Hong’en Lingji Palace

We reflect that since ancient times, the worthy and wise have been born as eminent ministers, manifesting merit in sheltering the people, and after death, as luminous spirits, aiding the transformations of Heaven and Earth. Their influence surges and flows between the two realms, bestowing blessings upon the people, warding off disasters, and averting calamities. They revive the withered and quicken the dead, their numinous presence vast and boundless, as if ever above, ever beside—beyond the reach of human wit to fathom.

The poem says: “The spirit’s advent—how can it be measured? How could it be spurned?” This is what it means.

Consider the Nine Heavens Golden Palace Illuminating Dao and Manifesting Virtue Great Immortal, Manifest Numinous and Universally Beneficent Perfected, Jiang Wang, and the Nine Heavens Jade Palace Propagating Transformation and Upholding the Dao Supreme Immortal, Manifest Numinous and Vastly Beneficent Perfected, Rao Wang—their merit and virtue overflow, truly as described above.

The spirits were scions of the Southern Tang, born with meritorious service to the people and the state, their names recorded in the registers of the immortal ranks. The elder and younger brothers, equal in splendor, their blossoms joined in fragrance. They illuminate their offices in the heavens above and manifest their numinous presence upon the earth below, their breath and response swift as lightning and wind. The people below are enveloped in their care, relying upon them as parents—compassionate, benevolent, and loving, all things depend on them for peace.

Recently, when We fell ill, We tried every remedy, yet none proved effective. The spirits silently mobilized their divine essence, guarding and protecting Our person. In an instant, without delay, they responded to every invocation, repeatedly displaying clear signs and bestowing sacred talismans. With celestial physicians and wondrous medicines, they restored Us from peril to safety, from collapse to recovery, achieving the merit of reviving the dead. Their grace and favor are vast indeed.

We measure Our virtue as meager and shallow—how could We have merited such blessings? We shall forever cherish their power, how could it ever be forgotten? Deep as the sea, towering as the mountains—where is their limit? Since repaying merit is the enduring principle of the state, We now issue this proclamation, conferring upon them additional divine titles: the elder shall be called *Pure Subtlety, Cavernous Mystery, Tranquil Emptiness, Wondrous Response, Compassionate and Benevolent, Vastly Gracious Perfected*; the younger shall be called *Lofty Clarity, Vast Stillness, Tranquil Profundity, Wondrous Response, Benevolent and Gracious, Vastly Gracious Perfected*. Their former titles remain unchanged.

We have newly constructed grand temples, bright and spacious, befitting the spirits’ abode. We command the relevant officials to maintain reverent and pure incense fires, to offer seasonal sacrifices in spring and autumn, to replace their robes with the changing seasons, and to appoint five households for sweeping and cleaning. Thus do We express Our sincerity and gratitude, repaying the spirits’ vast blessings.

Though the spirits’ merit is profound, Our repayment is meager. Yet the spirits surely do not demand repayment—still, in Our heart, We cannot cease. The virtue of heaven and earth is life; the spirits embody the Dao of heaven and earth, assisting and illuminating the mechanisms of transformation. They emerge from darkness and enter obscurity, expanding and contracting in an instant, roaming the Great Clarity, overseeing the four directions, and benefiting all living beings. Their merit spans heaven and earth, so radiant that it cannot be fully described in words.

Thus do We make manifest their achievements, inscribing them upon unyielding stone, erecting them in their temples to be displayed for eternity. We append a poem:

Heaven bore heroic spirits, paragons of the age,
Moving through darkness and light as though in a single glance.
In life, their merit protected the land of Ou and Yue;
In death, as luminous spirits, their grandeur endures.
Vast as the sea, beneath flowering trees they rest,
Regulating rain and shine, cold and heat with perfect measure.
Winter knows no biting frost, summer no scorching heat,
Cool breezes dispel distant pestilence, no gales assail.
Lowlands yield abundant grain, high fields teem with millet,
Driving away misfortune, banishing demons and serpents.
Morning bells and evening drums gladden the young and old,
Atop towering Ao Peak, steep and majestic.
Winding and embracing, shaped by heaven’s design,
Spring orchids offer fragrance, autumn chrysanthemums bloom.
Cold springs and white stones, pure and pristine,
The spirits roam with feathered canopies.
Flying clouds, fluttering Dao banners and standards,
Swiftly they come and go, their numinous presence tireless.
Breath and response swift as verse,
Their boundless beneficence knows no end.
Four seasons bring offerings, rich libations,
Their protective power rivals none.
We vow to inscribe this in heart and bone,
Engraving their virtue upon enduring stone,
To display for ten thousand ages, radiant as sun and moon.

The fifteenth day of the fifth month, the fifteenth year of the Yongle reign.
*Filial Piety and Sincere Response Record*

Juror Zhu Guanyinbao of the First Ward, Zuan Pearl Craftsmen’s Quarter, Waxie Dam, Jiangning County, Ying Tian Prefecture, repeatedly dreamed from the eleventh month of the sixteenth year of the Yongle reign of his mother at home, now laughing, now speaking, filling Guanyinbao’s heart with longing. On the twenty-third day of the twelfth month, a familiar acquaintance, Zhang De’gang, arrived from Nanjing at Guanyinbao’s lodging and reported: “When I left, your mother was at home with a sore on the back of her head that had not yet healed.” Upon hearing this, Guanyinbao was stricken with grief and alarm, unable to compose himself. He immediately vowed before heaven to journey to the Hong’en Lingji Palace and perform three thousand prostrations—one thousand to repay heaven and earth, one thousand to repay the king, and one thousand to pray for talismanic medicine, beseeching the protection of his mother’s peace.

At dawn on the twenty-fifth day, he began his prostrations at the Hong’en Lingji Palace. Upon completing the first thousand, he lit three sticks of incense on his right arm and recited the Jade Emperor’s sacred name one hundred and eight times. As he neared the end, suddenly white clouds surged forth, filling the hall. A divine figure appeared, towering in stature, his face white as jade, his beard and whiskers resplendent. He wore a golden *ruyi* crown and a Daoist robe woven with gold, standing amidst the clouds and beckoning to Guanyinbao. Overcome with awe, Guanyinbao prostrated himself before the deity, who then bestowed upon him a sprig of dried cypress leaves and two yellow paper talismans inscribed with vermilion gourd symbols, saying: “Take these and brew a decoction for your mother.” Guanyinbao received them with reverence, and as he rose from his bow, the divine figure vanished without a trace. Cold sweat drenched his body, and he collapsed in a faint. Only with the aid of the palace’s Daoist attendants did he revive, whereupon he completed the remaining two thousand prostrations.

The following day, his vows fulfilled, the palace’s Daoist attendants escorted him to report the matter to the authorities. By order of the Ministry of Rites, he was granted five thousand strings of cash, ten *shi* of rice, exemption from corvée labor, and commendation for his filial piety, with a horse provided for his return to attend his mother.

We note that among the conduct of mortals, nothing surpasses loyalty and filial piety. When loyalty and filial devotion reach their utmost, they move the gods and spirits, as seen in the case of Zhu’s son. In his daily life, his thoughts were ever fixed on loyalty and filial piety. Upon hearing of his mother’s illness, his grief and urgency drove him to the Lingji Palace, where he extolled the sacred name and paid homage to the Perfected Lord. In that fleeting moment of reverie, he beheld the descent of the deity, who bestowed upon him talismanic medicine—how could this have been achieved without the power of his loyalty and filial devotion?

The nature of the divine lies in its responsiveness to breath and invocation, its transformations beyond measure. Moreover, the Perfected Lord, having attained the Dao and ascended as an immortal, enters the realm of gods and transcends the mundane, wielding the mechanisms of creation, protecting the state and sheltering the people. His monumental merits and boundless virtue shine resplendent between heaven and earth, and his responsive efficacy defies description. By imperial decree, he was granted a special honorific title, and a shrine was erected in his name, where seasonal sacrifices are offered—a ritual of veneration unmatched in ancient or modern times.

Through Zhu’s son’s prayer, the Perfected Lord’s divine response was made manifest, proving that even the sincerity of a common man or woman is sufficient to move heaven and earth, let alone the gods. Yet this is not merely a testament to Zhu’s son’s devotion; all who pray with utmost sincerity shall receive a response. If one’s heart lacks sincerity, not only will the gods remain unmoved, but retribution may follow. How can one not be cautious? How can one not be vigilant?

*Auspicious Omens*

When the Lingji Palace was first established in the capital, on the eleventh day of the first lunar month in the sixteenth year of the Yongle reign of the Great Ming (the year *wuxu*), five-colored auspicious radiance appeared upon the banner poles. On the twelfth day, five-colored auspicious radiance again manifested upon the banner poles. On the fourteenth day, as the Shangyuan Festival rites were conducted, three cranes circled before the palace, and five-colored auspicious radiance once more appeared upon the banner poles. On the fifteenth day, four cranes circled before the palace, and five-colored radiance manifested again.

On the seventeenth day of the fourth lunar month of the same year, during the sacred festival when an altar was erected for the offering of vegetarian repasts and prayers for longevity, four cranes circled before the palace from afar. On the ninth day of the first lunar month of the following year, the *Jinlu Baoyan Yanxi Luotian* Grand Offering was conducted. At the *chen* hour on the eleventh day, five-colored auspicious radiance appeared upon the main hall, the front hall, the three gates, the Lingxing Gate, the left and right banner poles, and the summit of the *hu* vessels. Auspicious clouds in the form of phoenixes gathered, and a jeweled canopy took shape. Azure and white auspicious radiance then manifested, sheltering the ritual altar, while a black crow alighted atop the right banner pole.

From the *wei* to the *shen* hours of the same day, five-colored felicitous clouds appeared above the main hall and the right banner pole, extending to shield the ritual altar. At the *chen* hour on the twelfth day, azure and white auspicious radiance manifested upon the banner poles and the jeweled canopy. At the *chen* hour on the thirteenth day, five-colored sacred radiance appeared upon the banner poles, the Lingxing Gate, and the summit of the *hu* vessels. Around the *wu* hour on the fourteenth day, azure, red, and yellow auspicious radiance manifested upon the banner poles, and five-colored felicitous clouds coalesced into the forms of dragons and phoenixes, encircling and protecting the ritual altar. At the *wei* hour, four cranes circled the altar from beyond the Heavenly Gate. Around the *shen* hour, azure, red, and yellow auspicious radiance appeared upon the banner poles and the jeweled canopy. Upon the left and right canopies, two small banners of royal hue naturally formed into longevity sashes.

At the *chen* hour on the fifteenth day, five-colored auspicious radiance manifested upon the left and right banner poles of the Lingxing Gate and the jeweled canopy atop the *hu* vessels. At the *si* hour, five-colored felicitous clouds sheltered the ritual altar. At the *wu* hour, cranes circled before the hall.

The Minister of War at the temporary court, Fang Bin, and the Commander of the Embroidered Uniform Guard, Lin Guan, respectfully memorialized the auspicious omens. On the ninth day of the first lunar month in the seventeenth year of the Yongle reign, by imperial command, the *Jinlu* Grand Vegetarian Rite was conducted at the Hong’en Lingji Palace. Since its commencement, the skies have been clear and the *qi* luminous, with five-colored auspicious radiance appearing for days on end. Auspicious clouds of resplendent hues, like soaring dragons and phoenixes, have enveloped the ritual altar. Cranes have arrived, black crows have descended, and upon the left and right banner poles and jeweled canopies, colored banners have naturally formed into longevity sashes. All these manifestations arise from the Emperor’s utmost filial piety and pure sincerity, which have moved heaven, earth, and the divine, bringing forth these auspicious signs in profusion—a true omen of the nation’s enduring prosperity for ten thousand myriad years and an inexhaustible blessing for all under heaven.

The Daoist officials and priests, with reverence, hereby report as follows:

The heart of man can move heaven and earth, stir spirits and gods, and penetrate metal and stone. Though heaven and earth are vast and difficult to move, though spirits and gods are subtle and difficult to stir, though metal and stone are hard and difficult to penetrate, that which can move, stir, and penetrate them is none other than utmost sincerity.

We revere the Perfected Lords of Vast Grace—
the Great Immortal of the Nine Heavens’ Golden Gate, who embodies the luminous Dao and the penetrating virtue, whose numinous efficacy is universal and whose subtle wisdom is profound;
and the Supreme Immortal of the Nine Heavens’ Jade Gate, who propagates transformation and upholds the teaching, whose numinous brilliance is vast and whose tranquil profundity is wondrous—
